                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cobalt+Oxalate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cobalt%20oxalate">cobalt oxalate</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Conversion&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_conversion">conversion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium">sodium</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_battery">battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries, the most common energy storage devices, are recently facing difficulties in supply resulting from the limited reserve of lithium resources and surge of lithium prices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Accordingly, abundant alkali elements such as sodium are considered the next available charge carrier to replace lithium. Besides, for the larger ionic size of sodium (1.02 Å) versus that of lithium (0.76 Å) and the operation voltages, the basic chemistries of sodium and lithium are similar, with both alkali ion carriers undergoing reactions via insertion, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> conversion, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> and alloying. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Because of the low melting point of sodium (98 °C) relative to that of lithium (168 °C), more care is necessary to prevent dendrite growth of sodium metal on the anode, which is a critical concern for commercial availability. Hard carbon is the most common anode material used for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) because the main electrochemical reaction progresses via insertion of sodium ions into disordered graphene layers to provide stable cyclability for prolonged cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> However, the intrinsic disordered nature of hard carbon tends to disturb the facile diffusion of sodium ions at high rates. Although improvement of the rate performance has been observed in other layered compounds that are activated by an insertion process, the higher operation voltage and smaller capacity relative to those of hard carbon dilute the advantage of the high rate capability. De-/alloying compounds have the merit of high capacity; however, the bulky volume change during the de-/alloying process causes exfoliation of electrode materials from the current collector. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> As a result, the capacity fading during the tested duration is drastic. For these reasons, much attention has been paid to conversion materials such as hydrides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> fluorides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 17 to 23">(17−23)</a> nitrides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> and phosphides. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Among these materials, metal oxides have been the most widely investigated as conversion anode materials for SIBs because of the multielectron reaction that leads to a high capacity over 300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which is similar to that of hard carbon at low rates. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> In general, the suggested reaction process of metal oxides can be represented as follows: MeO + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>–</sup> → Me + Na<sub>2</sub>O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Similarly, metal fluoride is converted as follows: MeF<sub>2</sub> + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>–</sup> → Me + 2NaF. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> The formations of Na<sub>2</sub>O and NaF do not have a positive effect on cell performance because they are electrical insulators such that electron transfer is interrupted by these insulating compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> Also, metal sulfides such as ZnS, FeS, and FeS<sub>2</sub> exhibit excellent sodium-ion storage capacity and reversible storage capacity in the following reaction: MeS + <i>n</i>Na<sup>+</sup> + <i>n</i>e<sup>–</sup> → Me + <i>n</i>NaS. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a> However, the material is sensitive against moisture and has low performance at high current density because of its rock salt-based structure.</div><div class="NLM_p last">A transition metal oxalate, as a new member of conversion-type electrode materials, is proposed as a possible anode material for SIBs. Although there are no reports on metal oxalates as anode materials for SIBs, some earlier studies demonstrated the possibility of lithium storage in Li cells, which the properties of the materials were compared to each other (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 30 to 34">(30−34)</a> Applying the concept to the Na system, it is anticipated that the following conversion reaction may occur in Na cells: MeC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>–</sup> → NaC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + Me (Me: transition metal). One of the advantages of the formation of NaC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is that Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is less insulative than Na<sub>2</sub>O and NaF. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Additionally, NaC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> has a less dispersive character than NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Our preliminary screening did not give the electrochemical activities for most transition metal oxalates in Na cells (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Fortunately, we observed that cobalt oxalate (CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) is the only compound that shows feasibility for sodium storage. Because electron transfer is important in conversion electrodes, CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is modified with electroconducting carbon and acetylene black in this study. The composite electrode (C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) gives a first charge (oxidation) capacity of about 330 mA h (g-CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<sup>−1</sup> at a rate of 0.2 C (60 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) with a first Coulombic efficiency of 67.1%. The C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode is active even at 30 C (9000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), still delivering a capacity of 111 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. This outstanding electrode performance is reported for the first time for several transition metal oxalate compounds tested in Na cells. A full cell comprised of the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode and carbon-coated NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathode demonstrates good retention capacity over prolonged cycling, delivering about 84.7% of the initial capacity [107 mA h (g-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>)<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C = 120 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which is set for the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathode] for 300 cycles and also delivering a capacity of 79.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a high rate (5 C = 550 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). Structural investigation leads to the proposal of a new reversible sodium storage mechanism: CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is converted into Co metal accompanied by the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> on discharge (reduction), and the Co metal is recovered to CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> on charge. Herein, we introduce the novelties of oxalate-based conversion anode materials and elucidate the associated reaction process for sodium storage.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Material Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p">CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was synthesized via a precipitation process. CoCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O (Kanto) and NaC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (Kanto) were first separately dissolved in distilled water, and both solutions were mixed together and then continuously stirred at 25 °C for 5 h. After the reaction, the precipitates were filtered and washed three times with deionized water and ethanol. The resultant product, CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O, was dried at 80 °C for 24 h in a vacuum oven.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Then, the as-received CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O powders were subjected to ball milling with acetylene black (10 wt % vs bare powder) for 5 h at 300 rpm. The ball-milled products were treated at 300 °C for 5 h in Ar. The carbon-free CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O was also heated under the same conditions to remove crystal water. The resultant was redried again under vacuum at 80 °C overnight.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Phases were identified using X-ray diffraction (XRD; 6 kW, X’Pert, PANalytical). The resulting patterns were refined using the FullProf Rietveld refinement program. Raman spectroscopy (inVia, Renishaw) was used to verify the presence of carbon in the produced compounds, and the carbon content was further quantified using a CHN analyzer (9.7 wt %). The thermal property was measured with thermogravimetric analysis (TGA). For the TGA (TGA; DTG-60, Shimadzu), the as-received CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O powders were heated from room temperature to 900 °C at a heating and a cooling rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in air. The surface area and porosity of cobalt oxalate were checked with an automated gas sorption analyzer (autosorb iQ, Quantachrome), which the details are described in our prior work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The direct current electrical conductivity was measured using the direct volt–ampere method (CMT-SR1000, AIT) and the sample was contacted with a four-point probe (pellet type: pellet, 0.785 cm<sup>2</sup> × 2.5 mm thickness). The products were examined using scanning electron microscopy (SEM; JSM 6400, JEOL) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (TEM; JEM-3010, JEOL).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Properties</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To fabricate electrodes, the as-synthesized and carbon composite powder were mixed with Super P as a conducting agent and polyacrylic acid in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone. Specifically, the total amounts of the active material, carbon, and binder were fixed at a ratio of 85:10:5; no additional carbon was used for the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode material. The slurry was applied onto the copper foil after drying it in an oven at 80 °C for 12 h. The loading mass of the active material was typically 4 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. To evaluate the electrochemical properties of the electrode, electrochemical tests were done in R2032 half cells using a Na metal anode. The electrolyte was 0.5 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a propylene carbonate/fluoroethylene carbonate (98:2 in volume) solution. For the full cell test, the cathode was fabricated by C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub> blended with Denka black and Super P as a conducting agent (1:1 by weight) and polyvinylidene fluoride (90:5:5 by weight). The C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathode and presodiated C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode were balanced with a capacity ratio of 1.2. Because of difficulty in the full cell configuration because of the large capacity difference between C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub> that shows about 120 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, we lowered the loading mass of C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to 3 mg cm<sup>–1</sup>, so that the resulting loading mass of C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub> was about 7.3 mg cm<sup>–1</sup> for the full cells.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Tested Electrodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Various properties (structure, morphology, and surface) of the tested electrodes were characterized by ex situ XRD, SEM, TEM, Raman spectroscopy, and time-of-flight secondary-ion mass spectroscopy (ToF-SIMS, PHI TRIFT V nanoTOF). Details of the sample preparation procedure are described in our prior work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The coprecipitation reaction resulted in formation of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O at 25 °C, and the compound was crystallized as an orthorhombic β-phase with the <i>C</i>2/<i>c</i> space group (<i>a</i> = 11.88 Å, <i>b</i> = 5.42 Å, <i>c</i> = 15.62 Å), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. The products were crystallized as nanorods with lengths of a few hundreds of nanometers but were agglomerated. It is thought that coprecipitation resulted in particle growth along a specific direction, as shown in the TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). Because CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O contains water molecules, we removed crystal water from the compound by heating, as almost all crystal water was observed to evaporate at 300 °C in an Ar atmosphere (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>); namely, CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O → CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O. The heating led to the formation of anhydrous cobalt oxalate, CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, with a monoclinic structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c inset). Rietveld refinement of the XRD pattern of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was carried out based on the <i>P</i>2<sub>1</sub>/<i>c</i> space group. The refined pattern matched well with the calculated one, with lattice constants <i>a</i> = 5.3286 Å, <i>b</i> = 5.6048 Å, <i>c</i> = 7.2322 Å, and β = 118.11° (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c bottom and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). The removal of crystal water led to transformation to a monoclinic structure. The crystal structure was reorganized to construct −C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>–Co–C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>–Co– chains or a layered structure consisting of alternate CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> tetrahedral layers. The TEM and SEM results confirmed decrease in the particle size of the nanorods (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>), and the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) results showed an increase in the specific surface area for CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>). The reduced particle size may shorten the ionic conduction path. More importantly, improvement in electron conduction is an important issue because facile electron transfer results in better electrode performances even for high rate operation. To fulfill these criteria, CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanorods were intimately mixed with acetylene black using ball milling, which did not alter the structure. The TEM and SEM image shows the presence of carbon together with nanorods (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>). The carbon in the composite was confirmed by the Raman spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f), specifically by the appearance of two vibrations at about 1247 and 1591 cm<sup>–1</sup>; the latter vibration is associated with the crystalline π-bond sp<sup>3</sup> character. In contrast, only the vibration related to CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was observed in the Raman spectrum for CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f). To confirm the effectiveness of the carbon addition, the electrical conductivity was measured for both CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and the CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>–acetylene black mixture (hereafter referred to as C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>). The electrical conductivity was greatly improved with the introduction of acetylene black from 2.8 × 10<sup>–7</sup> to 3.4 × 10<sup>–3</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, to confirm the capacity confirmation from acetylene black, the acetylene black electrode was tested, and the electrode did not show a good storing property for sodium ion (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13641y_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD pattern of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O (inset: structural model drawn based on the XRD pattern). (b) TEM image of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O (inset: the resulting SAED pattern) (c) Rietveld refinement results of as-synthesized bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (inset: as-synthesized powder image and structural model drawn based on the refinement results presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). TEM image in which the squared region is magnified in the inset of (d) bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and (e) C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, the resulting SAED pattern of (d-1) bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and (e-1) C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. (f) Raman spectra of bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and pitch carbon.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13641&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical activities for CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> were first investigated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) between 0 and 3 V at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). Although both electrodes showed irreversible reactions between cathodic and anodic sweeps, which is related to formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and associated reductive decomposition of the electrolyte at the first cycle, both CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrodes exhibited electrochemical activities in Na cells. It is worth mentioning, because it is the first finding of the electrochemical activity amongst transition metal oxalates as an anode material for SIBs. The associated electrochemical reaction will be mentioned in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. To confirm the electrochemical properties, the CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite electrodes were cycled in a voltage range of 0–3 V at 60 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (0.2 C, where 1 C: 300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) at 25 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). Both electrodes had a similar tendency for discharge (reduction) and charge (oxidation), with discharge capacities about 537 mA h (g-CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<sup>−1</sup> for the bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode and 541 mA h (g-CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<sup>−1</sup> for the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite electrode. The overcapacities, which are higher than the theoretical capacity (466 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) for both CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, at the first discharge are mainly ascribed to the formation of SEI and reductive decomposition, which causes high initial irreversibility. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Note that there was a large difference in the charge (oxidation) capacities: 251 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (46.9% efficiency) for the bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode and 330 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (67.1% efficiency) for the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode. The cycling stability was proved for long-term cyclability tested at a rate of 0.5 C after the first cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c,d); namely, after 200 cycles, almost no capacity, 0.09%, was delivered for the CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode, whereas 75.2% was delivered for the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode. Compared with the CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode, the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode showed significant improvement in rate capability. The cells were tested at different currents up to 30 C (9 A g<sup>–1</sup>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e–g). The obtained capacities were disappointing for the bare electrode, which delivered only 3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 C and even worse low capacity and retention at each rate. In contrast, the charge capacity was about 111 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even at 30 C for the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode, and the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode was cycled at 30 C and showed 42.4% after 100 cycles. The resulting performance of the present C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is better than that of other cobalt-based electrodes for sodium storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 39 to 46">(39−46)</a> It is likely that the synergetic features of the nanorod morphology shortening the conduction path combined with the intimate contact between electroconducting carbon and active nanorods efficiently improved the electron transport during continued cycles even at high rates.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13641y_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) CV of bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. Comparison of (b) the first charge and discharge curves of bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. (c) Charge and discharge curves of bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> during 200 cycles at room temperature and (d) resulting cyclability (blue line: Coulombic efficiency of C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>). (e) Rate capability of bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> at room temperature at different charge rates and (f) cyclability result of bare CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. (g) Cyclability results of C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> at 30 C current rate during 100 cycles (inset: charge and discharge curves of C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>). For rate tests, the applied currents for discharge and charge are identical (e–g).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13641&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13641y_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Phase evolution of the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite electrode upon sodiation (reduction: a-1,2,3) and desodiation (oxidation: a-4,5,6). (b) First discharge–charge curve of C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>; the applied current was 60 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (0.2 C) at 25 °C for the sample preparation. (b-1) TEM bright-field image of the as-prepared C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite and corresponding SAED pattern. (b-2) TEM bright-field image of the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite sodiated to 0 V and corresponding SAED pattern. (b-3) TEM bright-field image of the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite desodiated to 3 V and the corresponding SAED pattern.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13641&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13641y_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Raman spectra of the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite electrode upon sodiation (reduction: a-1,2,3) and desodiation (oxidation: a-4,5). (b) ToF-SIMS spectra of the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite electrode upon sodiation (reduction: b-1,2,3) and desodiation (oxidation: b-4,5); left first: NaC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup> positive fragment (<i>m</i> = 80.99), left second: NaC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup> positive fragment (<i>m</i> = 81.99), middle: NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup> positive fragment (<i>m</i> = 82.97), and right: C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>3</sub>H<sup>+</sup> positive fragment (<i>m</i> = 84.99). The applied current was 60 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (0.2 C) at 25 °C for the sample preparation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13641&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Further investigation was performed to comprehend the sodium storage process for the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrodes in terms of the bulk structure, morphology, and surface states. First, the discharge and charge behaviors were evaluated for C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> using XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). Compared with the fresh electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a-1), the relative intensity of the XRD pattern for CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> dramatically decreased, and a small peak corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> at about 31° (2θ) appeared after sodiation to 0.6 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a-2). This tendency was more dominant upon sodiation to 0 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a-3), and a peak corresponding to cubic Co metal appeared at 44.5° (2θ). At this stage, the nanorod morphology was no longer observed; however, the particles were agglomerated at the nanoscale, and the newly formed particle size was distributed below 40 nm in diameter (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b-2). The formation of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Co metal was evident in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern.</div><div class="NLM_p">As desodiation progressed, the formed Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Co metal were gradually diminished (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a-4). The resulting XRD and SAED patterns demonstrate that Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Co were rearranged into the CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> structure, although the corresponding relative intensity was greatly diminished compared with that of the fresh electrode after charging to 3 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a-6,b-3). In addition, the original nanorod morphology was not recovered after charge; however, the nanoparticle morphology observed at 0 V was maintained (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b-3). The weak and broad signal of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in the XRD pattern are related to the nanoscale particle size of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a-6). The formation of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was further confirmed in the SAED pattern, which corresponds to that of fresh CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d-1).</div><div class="NLM_p">The Raman spectroscopy and ToF-SIMS analyses provided more convincing results on the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> on sodiation (discharge) and recovery to CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> on desodiation (charge), as the relative intensities of these compounds were weak in the XRD patterns. The C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode in the fresh state showed typical vibrations of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a-1). The relative intensity of the CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> vibration was reduced for sodiation to 0.6 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a-2). Although the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was not as clear in either the XRD or Raman data, the ToF-SIMS results showed the clear appearance of the related positive fragment, NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup> (<i>m</i> = 82.97) at 0.6 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b-2), which implies the presence of the Na–C–O chemical bond. Note that such fragments were not observed in the fresh electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b-1). In addition, the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> is not possible at 0 V because of the reductive decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>O and CO<sub>2</sub> at this low voltage. The relative intensity of the fragment was highlighted at 0 V, which agrees with the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in the Raman spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a-3) and TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b-2). It is also thought that, because Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was formed on the active materials, the D- and G-bands related to carbon were simultaneously observed in the Raman spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a-3). From these results, the following related reaction on sodiation to 0 V is proposed<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13641y_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">On desodiation, it is clear that the vibrations related to CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> appeared again at 0.6 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a-4), whereas the relative intensity of the NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup> (<i>m</i> = 82.97) fragment was somehow reduced in the ToF-SIMS spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b-4). At the end of desodiation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b-5), the resulting vibrations were CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b-5), and the relative intensity of the NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup> (<i>m</i> = 82.97) fragment was significantly reduced (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b-5). These spectroscopic results agree with the XRD and TEM data. According to the TEM images, after charging to 3 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b-3), it was not possible to observe the nanorod morphology; however, the resultant products were agglomerates composed of nanoparticles. The Raman and ToF-SIMS results indicate that although the morphology changed to that of nanoparticle agglomerates, the main composition of the agglomerates was electrochemically induced CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> after charging to 3 V. The reaction is reversible. On the basis of these results, the following associated reaction is suggested<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13641y_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">This conversion reaction is reversible, such that the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was able to retain a capacity of about 73.2% of the first capacity for 200 cycles. XRD analysis of the cycled C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode further confirmed the stability of the electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). The XRD pattern verifies the presence of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> even after 200 cycles, which contain an extra peak of NaOH at 35.5° (2θ) by moisture contamination during the measurement. As observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b-2, the resulting SAED pattern verifies the formation of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. The difference in the molar volumes, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub>, of CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (7.1269 cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>) and Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (7.6151 cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>), is only 7% such that the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> may not lead to drastic exfoliation of the active materials from the Cu current collector or deformation of the electrode. This is considered a possible reason for the retention of the high capacity during the extensive cycling test.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13641y_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) XRD pattern of the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> composite electrode after 200 cycles and (b) resulting TEM image in which the squared region is the resulting SAED pattern for the inset of C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13641&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The long-term cyclability of the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode was tested in full cells fabricated by pairing the anode with carbon-coated NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathodes (hereafter referred to as C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub>). The detailed electrode performance is described in our prior work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> To improve the first Coulombic efficiency of the full cell, the C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode was electrochemically stabilized by three consecutive cycles. The N/P capacity ratio was controlled to a value of 1.2 based on the capacity of the cathode and anode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). The full cell exhibited an initial discharge capacity of about 107 mA h (g-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>)<sup>−1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a–c), and outstanding cycling stability at 1 C (110 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, based on the full cell capacity), retaining 84.7% of the initial capacity after 300 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). In addition, the capacity at a rate of 5 C (550 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) was about 80 mA h (g-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>)<sup>−1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). These test results indicate the possibility of using oxalate-based anode materials for sodium storage. The resulting energy density of the C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub>//C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> full cell was approximately 180 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> based on the weight of both cathode and anode, of which the value is comparable to other systems (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/suppl_file/am8b13641_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 48 to 51">(48−51)</a> that other full cells utilize different kinds of cathode and anode materials.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13641y_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Voltage profiles of the C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathode and presodiated C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode tested in half cells and C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub>//C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> full cell. (b) Discharge profile vs cycle number at different current rates (0.1–5 C) of the C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub>//C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> full cell (inset: discharge curves vs cycle number). (c) Schematic illustration of the C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub>//C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> full cell.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13641/asset/images/large/am-2018-13641y_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13641&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The high-capacity C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrode shows feasibility of sodium storage via a reversible conversion reaction as follows: CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 2Na<sup>+</sup> → Co + Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. In addition, the employed electroconducting carbon is likely to provide good electron conduction paths, which will enable fast conversion on both discharge and charge. The C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>//C–NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cells show acceptable cell performances in SIBs that was able to retain a capacity of about 84.7% of the initial capacity [107 mA h (g-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>)<sup>−1</sup>] for 300 cycles and are active even at a rate of 5 C (550 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, based on the full cell capacity), delivering a capacity of 79.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The feasibility of using C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> electrodes for SIBs from a practical standpoint was also confirmed.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, SEM–energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy spectrum of C–CoC<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, electrochemical property of the acetylene black electrode, and comparison for the                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly ordered mesoporous NiO anode material for lithium ion batteries with an excellent electrochemical performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Hao; Wang, Guoxiu; Liu, Jian; Qiao, Shizhang; Ahn, Hyojun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we have synthesized highly ordered mesoporous NiO materials by a nanocasting method using mesoporous silica KIT-6 as the hard templates.  Mesoporous NiO particles were characterized by small angle x-ray diffraction, nitrogen adsorption/desorption, and transmission electron microscopy.  The results demonstrated that the as-prepd. mesoporous NiO had an ordered Ia3d sym. mesostructure, with a high surface area of 96 m2/g.  Mesoporous NiO materials were tested as an anode material for lithium-ion batteries, exhibiting much lower activation energy (20.75 kJ/mol) compared to the bulk NiO (45.02 kJ/mol).  We found that the mesoporous NiO electrode has higher lithium intercalation kinetics than its bulk counterpart.  The sp. capacity of mesoporous NiO after 50 cycles was 680 mA-h/g at 0.1C, which was much higher than that of the com. bulk NiO (188 mA-h/g).  Furthermore, at a high rate of 2C, the discharge capacity of mesoporous NiO was as high as 515 mA-h/g, demonstrating the potential to be used for high-power lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoDRdP-s0KSNLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liVEnTO4JFVaQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhvFCitLg%253D&amp;md5=9112d5c9634b902b5e84d50777e426d6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200320"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vu, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stein, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Porous Electrode Materials for Lithium-ion Batteries-How to prepare them and what makes them special</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Porous electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries - how to prepare them and what makes them special</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Vu, Anh; Qian, Yuqiang; Stein, Andreas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Numerous benefits of porous electrode materials for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been demonstrated, including examples of higher rate capabilities, better cycle lives, and sometimes greater gravimetric capacities at a given rate compared to nonporous bulk materials.  These properties promise advantages of porous electrode materials for LIBs in elec. and hybrid elec. vehicles, portable electronic devices, and stationary elec. energy storage.  This review highlights methods of synthesizing porous electrode materials by templating and template-free methods and discusses how the structural features of porous electrodes influence their electrochem. properties.  A section on electrochem. properties of porous electrodes provides examples that illustrate the influence of pore and wall architecture and interconnectivity, surface area, particle morphol., and nanocomposite formation on the utilization of the electrode materials, specific capacities, rate capabilities, and structural stability during lithiation and delithiation processes.  Recent applications of porous solids as components for three-dimensionally interpenetrating battery architectures are also described.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrR3Y5G3fAhCLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhXIoa0BRmhCA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsFags7rO&amp;md5=85d4c67c1d9ac4beee04147e773fef4e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201002724"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takahashi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoon, C.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanostructured TiO2 and Its Application in Lithium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Myung, Seung-Taek; Takahashi, Naohiro; Komaba, Shinichi; Yoon, Chong Seung; Sun, Yang-Kook; Amine, Khalil; Yashiro, Hitoshi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Titania nanorods and nanowires are synthesized via a hydrothermal reaction of amorphous TiO2 in alk. NaOH, followed by ion exchange in HCl aq. soln., and dehydration at 400 °C.  Although the hydrothermal treatment produces three different particle morphologies depending on the reaction time (nanosheets, nanorods, and nanowires), the products exhibit the same crystal structure.  Ion exchange of Na2Ti3O7 in HCl aq. soln. brings about a phase change to H2Ti3O7, but there is no change in the particle morphol.  Dehydration of the nanostructured H2Ti3O7 leads to two types of crystal structure-anatase TiO2 for the nanorods, and TiO2-B for the nanowires-although no significant difference is found in the morphol. of the products even after dehydration.  The nanorods are 40-50 nm in length and 10 nm in diam., whereas the nanowires are several micrometers in length and tens to hundreds of nanometers in thickness.  In-situ X-ray diffraction revealed the formation of anatase TiO2 from the TiO2-B above 450 °C.  This finding implies that the phase transformation occurs rather slowly for the TiO2-B nanowires due to the larger particle size and higher crystallinity of H2Ti3O7.  Tests with Li-metal half cells indicated that the anatase TiO2 nanorods are more favorable for the storage and release of Li ions because of their greater surface area than the TiO2-B nanowires.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo21FeXjpt3c7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhXIoa0BRmhCA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXosVSjsLo%253D&amp;md5=8d5a685ba9a0d344012332e56b49bbca</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee41960f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kikuchi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoon, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The low elec. cond. of α-MnS generally impedes its use as an electrode material for sodium-ion batteries.  However, in this study, an unprecedentedly high capacity and long-term cyclability were achieved for an α-MnS electrode at moderate and high rates by introducing electro-conductive carbon surfaces.  A capacity of 302 mA h g-1 was delivered in the first cycle with 85% capacity retention after 200 cycles at 100 mA g-1.  Furthermore, the cycling performance at 10C (6.1 A g-1) demonstrated the feasibility of high-rate sodium storage using this anode, with 78% retention of the initial capacity after 200 cycles.  This performance was reproduced in a NaCrO2/α-MnS full cell as well.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel low-temp. non-aq. synthesis based on ionic liq. medium is used to fabricate well-defined iron-based fluoride nanomaterias.  This work demonstrates microstructural optimization which contributes pos. to both the large reversible Li-storage capacity and the high reactive voltage of carbon-free FeF3.0.33H2O at room temp.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Confinement of nanosized tin(IV) oxide particles on rGO sheets and its application to sodium-ion full cells as a high capacity anode material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jo, Chang-Heum; Jo, Jae-Hyeon; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report the synthesis and electrochem. reactions of nanosized SnO2/reduced graphene oxide (rGO), as well as its cell performance that implement full cells coupled with C-coated NaCrO2 cathodes.  The authors synthesize nanosized SnO2/rGO composites to mitigate main drawback that conversion and alloy reaction materials suffer from self-pulverization on discharge (redn.).  Hydrothermally produced SnO2 nanoparticles are simultaneously attached onto rGO sheets via a self-assembly process, in which rGO sheets provide sufficient electron conduction paths (∼10-3 S cm-1) during electrochem. reactions.  As anticipated, this technique results in satisfactory cell performance with help from the effect mentioned above.  For the 1st time, the authors apply the SnO2/rGO composite materials to a full cell, adopting a C-coated NaCrO2 (110 mA h (g-NaCrO2)-1) cathode.  The full cell demonstrates an excellent capacity retention, ∼84% of the initial capacity (88 mA-h (g-NaCrO2)-1) for 300 cycles, and is active even at a rate of 10C (1.05 A g-1), delivering 87 mA-h (g-NaCrO2)-1.  This result demonstrates the feasibility of using C-coated NaCrO2//SnO2/rGO Na-ion cells for energy storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqGE-CKnTn4V7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgrsjdCmkphMw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs1OitL3L&amp;md5=7c63a49fd5f9485c6c8e7bfe079fde42</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/b705421c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Larcher, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Beattie, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morcrette, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Edström, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jumas, J.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent findings and prospects in the field of pure metals as negative electrodes for Li-ion batteries</span>. <i>J. In the race for better Li-ion batteries, research on anode materials is very intensive as there is a strong desire to find alternatives to carbonaceous anodes.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Size and Composition Effects in Sb-Carbon Nanocomposites for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ramireddy, Thrinathreddy; Sharma, Neeraj; Xing, Tan; Chen, Ying; Leforestier, Jeremie; Glushenkov, Alexey M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are in the spotlight as viable alternatives to lithium-ion batteries in stationary storage and power grid applications.  Among possible anode materials, Sb is one of the interesting candidates due to a combination of battery-type potential plateaus in the charge-discharge profiles, high capacity (theor. capacity of 660 mAh g-1), and demonstrated good cyclic stability.  The effect of Sb particle size (particularly at the nanoscale range) and the compn. of Sb-carbon composites on the electrode performance, stability, and charge storage mechanism is systematically evaluated here for the first time.  A range of Sb-carbon nanocomposites with varied Sb particle size (between 50 and ∼1 nm) are studied.  The control of the particle size is achieved via varying the carbon and Sb wt. ratio in the precursors.  The shape of charge-discharge profiles, hysteresis, and the difference in cyclic stabilities and rate performance are analyzed.  The nanocomposite with the smallest particle size (∼1 nm) and the largest carbon content provides the most stable cyclic behavior and a better rate capability but suffers from an increased hysteresis between charge and discharge curves.  In situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction expts. indicate that the storage mechanism in the Sb-carbon nanocomposites contg. Sb nanoparticles is different from the electrodes with bulkier, micron-sized Sb particles, and the electrochem. reaction proceeds through a no. of cryst. intermediates.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZu3AXRGlszLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgrsjdCmkphMw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhslSisbbF&amp;md5=dee25eed73684b841e7a3cbdfa71accc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1039/c3cc40448j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ahn, H.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">SnO<sub>2</sub>@graphene nanocomposites as anode materials for Na-ion batteries with superior electrochemical performance</span>. <i>Chem.                 10.3762/bjnano.6.186</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D1821-1839%26journal%3DBeilstein%2BJ.%2BNanotechnol.%26author%3DL.%2BAymard%26author%3DY.%2BOumellal%26author%3DJ.-P.%2BBonnet%26title%3DBeilstein%2BJ.%2BMetal%2Bhydrides%253A%2Ban%2Binnovative%2Band%2Bchallenging%2Bconversion%2Breaction%2Banode%2Bfor%2Blithium-ion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.3762%252Fbjnano.6.186&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b13641&amp;doiOfLink=10.3762%2Fbjnano.6.186&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Metal hydrides: an innovative and challenging conversion reaction anode for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Aymard, Luc; Oumellal, Yassine; Bonnet, Jean-Pierre</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Beilstein Journal of Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1821-1839</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">BJNEAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2190-4286</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Beilstein-Institut zur Foerderung der Chemischen Wissenschaften</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The state of the art of conversion reactions of metal hydrides (MH) with lithium is presented and discussed in this review with regard to the use of these hydrides as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries.  A focus on the gravimetric and volumetric storage capacities for different examples from binary, ternary and complex hydrides is presented, with a comparison between thermodn. prediction and exptl. results.  MgH2 constitutes one of the most attractive metal hydrides with a reversible capacity of 1480 mA·h·g-1 at a suitable potential (0.5 V vs Li+/Li0) and the lowest electrode polarization (&lt;0.2 V) for conversion materials.  Conversion process reaction mechanisms with lithium are subsequently detailed for MgH2, TiH2, complex hydrides Mg2MHx and other Mg-based hydrides.  The reversible conversion reaction mechanism of MgH2, which is lithium-controlled, can be extended to others hydrides as: MHx + xLi+ + xe- in equil. with M + xLiH.  Other reaction paths-involving solid solns., metastable distorted phases, and phases with low hydrogen content-were recently reported for TiH2 and Mg2FeH6, Mg2CoH5 and Mg2NiH4.  The importance of fundamental aspects to overcome technol. difficulties is discussed with a focus on conversion reaction limitations in the case of MgH2.  The influence of MgH2 particle size, mech. grinding, hydrogen sorption cycles, grinding with carbon, reactive milling under hydrogen, and metal and catalyst addn. to the MgH2/carbon composite on kinetics improvement and reversibility is presented.  Drastic technol. improvement in order to the enhance conversion process efficiencies is needed for practical applications.  The main goals are minimizing the impact of electrode vol. variation during lithium extn. and overcoming the poor electronic cond. of LiH.  To use polymer binders to improve the cycle life of the hydride-based electrode and to synthesize nanoscale composite hydride can be helpful to address these drawbacks.  The development of high-capacity hydride anodes should be inspired by the emergent nano-research prospects which share the knowledge of both hydrogen-storage and lithium-anode communities.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoD1b1a4IYmRbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liA6rjjLgSBFA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhs12hsL7N&amp;md5=bce842656656b3d6436db93ba41e141d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1016/s0378-7753(96)02580-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Arai, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okada, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sakurai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamaki, J.-i.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cathode performance and voltage estimation of metal trihalides</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure and Electrochemistry of Copper Fluoride Nanocomposites Utilizing Mixed Conducting Matrices</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Badway, F.; Mansour, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Near-theor. capacity (450 mA-h/g for the composite and 525 mA-h/g for pure CuF2) of high-energy-d. CuF2 pos. electrode materials for lithium batteries was obtained by the use of nanocomposites consisting of 2-30 nm domains of CuF2 within a mixed ionic + electronic conducting matrix of a metal oxide.  Small but significant crystallog. changes to the core crystal of the CuF2 occurred in all oxide-based matrixes.  These modifications to the core crystal and the surrounding matrix were investigated through a host of characterization methods, including XRD, XPS, and XAS.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Binder-free NiO@MnO2 core-shell electrode: Rod-like NiO core prepared through corrosion by oxalic acid and enhanced pseudocapacitance with sphere-like MnO2 shell</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Xu; Chen, Gang; Guan, Hongtao; Dong, Chengjun; Xiao, Xuechun; Wang, Yude</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">189</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">83-92</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Binder-free NiO@MnO2 core-shell structure was deposited on Ni foam used as electrode of supercapacitor.  The rod-like NiO core was prepd. by thermal decompn. of NiC2O4 synthesized by corrosion of Ni foam with oxalic acid under hydrothermal conditions.  Sphere-like MnO2 shell was deposited on the surface of NiO core through hydrothermal treatment with KMnO4 soln.  The structure, compn., morphol. evolution and surface chem. state of the NiO@MnO2 electrode were analyzed by XRD, SEM, energy dispersive X-ray (EDX), TEM and XPS.  The obtained NiO and NiO@MnO2 electrodes were directly used as electrodes of supercapacitor, and their electrochem. performances were tested with cyclic voltammograms, galvanostatic charge/discharge and electrochem. impedance spectroscopy.  The sphere-like MnO2 shell leads to an impressive enhancement of the performance including the areal capacitance (a.c.) and cycling stability, particular in the AC.  To be more specific, the NiO@MnO2 exhibits a high a.c. of 3.584 F/cm2 at a c.d. of 5 mA/cm2, which is much higher than that of NiO electrode whose a.c. is 1.483 F/cm2 at the same c.d., demonstrating the potential to use NiO@MnO2 electrode for supercapacitor.  At the same time, the related mechanisms for the superior performance are also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrWsPiu17ctJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljvJ2FuKZlilA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXitVynu7vN&amp;md5=61c64736c141fcb804c448c4ded5f199</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201500981"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guan, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A High-Capacity and Long-Cycle-Life Lithium-Ion Battery Anode Architecture: Silver Nanoparticle-Decorated SnO2/NiO Nanotubes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Chanhoon; Jung, Ji-Won; Yoon, Ki Ro; Youn, Doo-Young; Park, Soojin; Kim, Il-Doo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11317-11326</span>CODEN:
